We use pretty much all HAAS CNC machines for all of our Goodwin University Advanced Manufacturing programs for hands on instruction. We have the Northeast regional HAAS office 15 minutes from our university facitities.  We are SO fortunate here in central Connecticut to have a very large number of global scale companies that need folks with skills in the sort of high level CNC, material science, quality assurance using CMMâ€™s, and high level scanning and 3D print prototyping along with production all these companies engage in. Our programs are literally filled to capacity every semester now and we are now consistently getting inquiries from all around the country from companies just like Motion looking for highly qualified entry level employee candidates. Itâ€™s just awesome seeing the growth of this sort of advanced manufacturing here in the US destined for the US markets that reaches far beyond just the aerospace and medical industries who are making highly expensive products outside of the consumer markets.  The amount of consistently steady growth in consumer level manufacturing coming back to our shores here in the US to create and solidify a separation of the dependence on Chinese manufacturing is so damn satisfying to see happening. We see it growing so steady from the most important indicator which is the demand and search for properly educated/skilled employees. A lot of folks donâ€™t realize how many of these success stories like Cleetus is creating for himself all get started while working in a productive environment where a great idea is inspired and the person finds the motivation to go for it and take the leap to make your own company.  Successful partnerships like this can offer SO much creative satisfaction as well as a lot of terrific solutions. Congrats Cleetus for taking that next step up!! Most of these parts are better die cast, forged, stamped, or drawn. Especially that valve cover will weigh MUCH less being drawn from a thin sheet of aluminum. Also much cheaper to produce that way. The US has fallen in love with milling but in most cases it is just a way to sell overpriced fancy looking parts. If you are making more than a few prototypes there are much better ways to make most of things and only machine the finishing steps on a few of them. Better look into production machines instead of overpriced Haas prototyping mills for everything. You spend more on milling cutters in a month than a 500 Ton press costs.

I don't know if you guys have looked into this, but you can avoid hogging out a lot of these parts by starting with investment castings. The casting gets you 90% of the way, and then you just have to machine and finish the final touches. You can also make some crazy geometry pumps/chassis to minimize the assembled components at the machined level. Wall thicknesses can get down to .060 for some of the elbows you showed while including multiple bends in one part.

machining these days is probably the least impressive thing that goes on in a shop like this, once a cnc is set up with the cad files, its just load, press go, and unload. the time and effort that goes into making the cad design, testing the machining of that part, going back, tweak the design, test the machining again, testing the finished product, going back to the drawing board because it is not working as planned, testing the machining again, testing the part again to finally end up with a well machined and functioning part, that is where the magic is these days.
